B000EMGAOY|1.0|Let's face it, there are some bands that can continue to stay relevant throughout the course of a lengthy career (R.E.M., Depeche Mode, The Cure, and I'm sure Radiohead will have the staying power), some bands that knew when to pack it in before the well potentially dried up leaving them as shells of their former selves ... and then there are the bands that continue on long after their expiration date, long after they are capable of producing any relevant material.  Among these bands are Goo Goo Dolls, U2, and ... Red Hot Chili Peppers.<br /><br />To use a well-worn cliche, these Peppers are becoming quite mild in middle age.  By far the most exciting years for this band were the ones with Jack Irons on drums and the late Hillel Slovak on guitar (I urge all newbie RHCP fans to check out Freaky Styley and The Uplift Mofo Party Plan, pronto), the years when the band had an innovative sound, an energetic stage show and was the antidote to generic corporate rock.  After Slovak's untimely passing, the band recruited John Frusciante and cut two solid albums in the same vein, albeit a bit more calculated and mainstream.  Frusciante departed to focus on his bizarre solo recordings, and with new guitarist Dave Navarro the band moved away from funk and in a decidedly more hard rock direction with One Hot Minute, which, while far from their best album, was at least interesting and inspired.  Navarro eventually left (his style was an odd pairing with the other members of RHCP), and Frusciante returned. The next album, Californication, brought another change in direction - it marked the beginning of the metamorphosis of the Chilis from red hot to quite bland and mild. Yes, there were a couple of songs that hinted at the band's prior days of glory, but much of the album consisted of songs aimed at the soccer mom set.  This is a trend that continued even more with the next album, By The Way.  That sets the stage for the band's latest release, the double-disc Stadium Arcadium.<br /><br />Does the band improve and show the fire and inspiration of the same group that gave us \\"Me & My Friends\\", \\"True Men Don't Kill Coyotes\\" and \\"Fight Like A Brave\\"?  Alas, no. The only thing this group has in common with the group that recorded those songs is a name.  Stadium Arcadium offers a whole bunch of safe, gentle light pop songs that are pretty much all about love and about how great the state of California is.  That's right, Anthony Kiedis is still preaching and trying to convert the uninitiated on the wonders of California.  Anthony, if you are lacking inspiration this much where half of your songs are about California, perhaps you should just go work for the California Board of Tourism instead.  Making matters worse is that the energy and enthusiasm that the Peppers once displayed is all gone - what's left is a band content to repeat themselves over and over again, coming up with little more than rewrites of previous hits in the hopes that they can buy more mansions and luxury cars.<br /><br />It is often stated by RHCP's defenders that their critics can't handle change.  That is not true.  The reviews are taking the band to task for putting little effort into their albums for the sake of cashing in on the hapless consumer hoping for another Uplift or Blood Sugar Sex Magik.  Instead, we're getting the same tired light pop that has been done to death by tons of other bands and retreads of prior bland hits like \\"Scar Tissue\\" and \\"Under The Bridge\\", proving my point about this band - they're only in it for the money.  Essentially, you've got the ballad (\\"Snow (Hey Oh), \\"Stadium Arcadium\\"), the soft pop song (\\"Dani California\\", \\"Tell Me Baby\\"), and the toothless tame \\"rock\\" song (\\"Readymade\\", \\"Hump De Bump\\"). There is no change to be found - the band has recorded the same two or three songs over and over again since 1999. That's not evolution - that's regression.<br /><br />In summary: It disappoints me to say this about a band I once loved, but Red Hot Chili Peppers (or should we call them The Sweet Lollipops instead?) have worn out their welcome a long time ago.  They only remain popular because they write very similar, indistinguishable songs that are as catchy (and vapid) as commercial jingles.  All this weak music is only going to tarnish their legacy, as it is vastly inferior to their first six albums (yes, even One Hot Minute).  Throw them right into the dinosaur rock heap of bands that continued to record material that is obviously inferior to their earlier output, much like U2 and the Stones.  Basically, the dull artwork tells you all you need to know about the music within.  I suggest passing on this album unless you think Nickelback, The Fray, Maroon 5 and other shallow \\"lite rock\\" bands are geniuses. If you want to hear the Chilis that took the music industry by storm, check out s/t, Freaky or Uplift instead.

B000MCH5P4|4.0|\\"Babel\\" is the latest film from director Alejandro Gonzalez Inarritu, who also served as co-producer with Steve Golin and Jon Kilik.  The story begins in a desolate region in the Moroccan desert where two brainless boys decide to see how far a new rifle can fire by using a tour bus full of people as target practice.  One bullet critically injures an American woman (Cate Blanchett) who along with her husband (Brad Pitt) is recovering from the death of their infant.  This new tragedy follows a series of earlier tragedies (including the suicide of a Japanese woman) and begins another series of tragedies (including the deportation of the couple's illegal immigrant nanny, played beautifully by Adriana Barraza).<br /><br />With the loud, abrasive action jumping back and forth through time, and from place to place (Morocco, Japan, America, and Mexico), and from one story to another, this is an exhausting film to follow.  In one story, the two Moroccan boys and their dysfunctional family try to escape justice.  In a related, but separate story, the American couple (portrayed with great sincerity and passion by Pitt and Blanchett) struggle in a life-or-death situation aided by the incompetent, though sympathetic, locals in a nearby village.  Meanwhile in California, the couple's children are being taken care of by a loving Mexican nanny who foolishly takes the children with her nephew (Gael Garcia Bernal) to a wild wedding party across the border.  In yet another story, with the most tenuous of thematic threads to link them, a teenaged deaf-mute Japanese girl (Rinko Kikuchi) runs around the city without panties trying to lose her virginity.<br /><br />Each story reveals characters with communication problems:  The American couple can't talk about the baby's death or the husband's earlier desertion; the boys have never told their parents about their sister's incestuous peep shows, the nanny is not only ignorant of the English language, but of American laws; the Japanese girl, who besides having difficulty communicating with anyone who's not deaf, has a strange and strained relationship with her father.<br /><br />This raw, depressing drama earns its R rating by providing in-your-face depictions of violence, nudity, survival, desperation, fear, and isolation.  There's obviously some masterful storytelling and moving performances in \\"Babel,\\" but overall, it's not extremely enjoyable to watch.<br /><br />Leslie Halpern, author of [[ASIN:1589790642 Reel Romance: The Lovers' Guide to the 100 Best Date Movies]] and [[ASIN:0786415967 Dreams on Film: The Cinematic Struggle Between Art and Science]].ing difficulty communicating with anyone who's not deaf, has a strange and strained relationship with her father.
